Abstract: Transfer learning and knowledge distillation has recently gained a lot of attention in the deep learning community. One transfer approach, the student-teacher learning, has been shown to successfully create ``small'' student neural networks that mimic the performance of a much bigger and more complex ``teacher'' networks. In this paper, we investigate an extension to this approach and transfer from a non-neural-based machine learning pipeline as teacher to a neural network (NN) student, which would allow for joint optimization of the various pipeline components and a single unified inference engine for multiple ML tasks. In particular, we explore replacing the random forest classifier by transfer learning to a student NN. We experimented with various NN topologies on 100 OpenML tasks in which random forest has been one of the best solutions. Our results show that for the majority of the tasks, the student NN can indeed mimic the teacher if one can select the right NN hyper-parameters. We also investigated the use of random forest for selecting the right NN hyper-parameters.

</details>


### [206] [A Practical Guide Towards Interpreting Time-Series Deep Clinical Predictive Models: A Reproducibility Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.24828)
*Yongda Fan,John Wu,Andrea Fitzpatrick,Naveen Baskaran,Jimeng Sun,Adam Cross*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A benchmark study assesses interpretability methods across clinical tasks and model architectures, finding attention can be efficient for explanations, black-box methods (KernelSHAP/LIME) are often infeasible for time-series tasks, and several methods are unreliable; proposes guidelines and provides PyHealth for reproducibility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address gaps in extensibility, reproducibility, and cross-task/architecture generalization of interpretable ML in clinical settings; answer whether architectural features like attention improve explainability and whether interpretability methods generalize across tasks.

Method: Development of a comprehensive benchmark evaluating multiple interpretability methods across diverse clinical prediction tasks and model architectures; systematic analysis of how interpretability varies with task-architecture interplay; implementation released via PyHealth (open-source) to support reproducibility and extension.

Result: Key findings: (1) Properly leveraged attention is a highly efficient and faithful explainer for model predictions; (2) Black-box explainers like KernelSHAP and LIME are computationally infeasible for time-series clinical predictions; (3) Several interpretability approaches are unreliable for trustworthy use.

Conclusion: Guidelines to improve interpretability in clinical predictive pipelines are proposed; the authors provide an open-source framework (PyHealth) to support reproducible benchmarking and extensibility of interpretability methods across clinical tasks and architectures.

Abstract: Clinical decisions are high-stakes and require explicit justification, making model interpretability essential for auditing deep clinical models prior to deployment. As the ecosystem of model architectures and explainability methods expands, critical questions remain: Do architectural features like attention improve explainability? Do interpretability approaches generalize across clinical tasks? While prior benchmarking efforts exist, they often lack extensibility and reproducibility, and critically, fail to systematically examine how interpretability varies across the interplay of clinical tasks and model architectures. To address these gaps, we present a comprehensive benchmark evaluating interpretability methods across diverse clinical prediction tasks and model architectures. Our analysis reveals that: (1) attention when leveraged properly is a highly efficient approach for faithfully interpreting model predictions; (2) black-box interpreters like KernelSHAP and LIME are computationally infeasible for time-series clinical prediction tasks; and (3) several interpretability approaches are too unreliable to be trustworthy. From our findings, we discuss several guidelines on improving interpretability within clinical predictive pipelines. To support reproducibility and extensibility, we provide our implementations via PyHealth, a well-documented open-source framework: https://github.com/sunlabuiuc/PyHealth.

### [207] [Reaching Beyond the Mode: RL for Distributional Reasoning in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.24844)
*Isha Puri,Mehul Damani,Idan Shenfeld,Marzyeh Ghassemi,Jacob Andreas,Yoon Kim*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Proposes a multi-answer reinforcement learning framework enabling LMs to emit multiple candidate answers in one forward pass, improving diversity, coverage, and calibration for tasks with inherent uncertainty, while reducing tokens and boosting coding accuracy; offers a compute-efficient alternative to best-of-k sampling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-world tasks often have multiple valid answers or irreducible uncertainty; standard post-training collapse to a single mode limits coverage and confidence estimation; need for distributional reasoning and efficient generation of multiple hypotheses.

Method: Introduce a modified RL objective that trains the model to generate multiple answers per prompt in a single forward pass, embedding search-like inference into the model's generation process; compare to single-answer baselines and inference-time methods.

Result: Across QA, medical diagnostics, and coding benchmarks, improved diversity, set-level calibration, and coverage; reduced token cost to produce multiple answers; coding tasks show higher accuracy.

Conclusion: Multi-answer RL provides a principled, compute-efficient alternative to inference-time scaling methods like best-of-k, enabling robust multi-hypothesis generation with confidence estimation in LMs.

Abstract: Given a question, a language model (LM) implicitly encodes a distribution over possible answers. In practice, post-training procedures for LMs often collapse this distribution onto a single dominant mode. While this is generally not a problem for benchmark-style evaluations that assume one correct answer, many real-world tasks inherently involve multiple valid answers or irreducible uncertainty. Examples include medical diagnosis, ambiguous question answering, and settings with incomplete information. In these cases, we would like LMs to generate multiple plausible hypotheses, ideally with confidence estimates for each one, and without computationally intensive repeated sampling to generate non-modal answers. This paper describes a multi-answer reinforcement learning approach for training LMs to perform distributional reasoning over multiple answers during inference. We modify the RL objective to enable models to explicitly generate multiple candidate answers in a single forward pass, internalizing aspects of inference-time search into the model's generative process. Across question-answering, medical diagnostic, and coding benchmarks, we observe improved diversity, coverage, and set-level calibration scores compared to single answer trained baselines. Models trained with our approach require fewer tokens to generate multiple answers than competing approaches. On coding tasks, they are also substantially more accurate. These results position multi-answer RL as a principled and compute-efficient alternative to inference-time scaling procedures such as best-of-k. Code and more information can be found at https://multi-answer-rl.github.io/.

</details>


### [208] [CVA: Context-aware Video-text Alignment for Video Temporal Grounding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.24934)
*Sungho Moon,Seunghun Lee,Jiwan Seo,Sunghoon Im*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: CVA introduces Query-aware Context Diversification (QCD), Context-invariant Boundary Discrimination (CBD) loss, and Context-enhanced Transformer Encoder (CTE) to improve timing-sensitive video-text alignment in VTG, achieving SOTA on QVHighlights and Charades-STA with ~5% Recall@1 gains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The VTG task struggles with irrelevant background context and fragile boundary localization, leading to false negatives and context-shift sensitivity. The work aims to make video-text alignment temporally aware, robust to context, and scalable across multi-scale temporal contexts.

Method: QCD creates a similarity-guided pool of replacement clips to diversify context while avoiding false negatives. CBD loss is a contrastive objective enforcing semantic consistency at difficult temporal boundaries. CTE is a hierarchical transformer using windowed self-attention and bidirectional cross-attention with learnable queries to capture multi-scale temporal context.

Result: Achieves state-of-the-art performance on VTG benchmarks QVHighlights and Charades-STA, with approximately 5-point improvement in Recall@1 over prior methods, demonstrating reduced false negatives and robust context handling.

Conclusion: The combination of data-centric (QCD) and architectural (CBD, CTE) enhancements yields robust, temporally aware video-text alignment, elevating VTG performance across benchmarks.

Abstract: We propose Context-aware Video-text Alignment (CVA), a novel framework to address a significant challenge in video temporal grounding: achieving temporally sensitive video-text alignment that remains robust to irrelevant background context. Our framework is built on three key components. First, we propose Query-aware Context Diversification (QCD), a new data augmentation strategy that ensures only semantically unrelated content is mixed in. It builds a video-text similarity-based pool of replacement clips to simulate diverse contexts while preventing the ``false negative" caused by query-agnostic mixing. Second, we introduce the Context-invariant Boundary Discrimination (CBD) loss, a contrastive loss that enforces semantic consistency at challenging temporal boundaries, making their representations robust to contextual shifts and hard negatives. Third, we introduce the Context-enhanced Transformer Encoder (CTE), a hierarchical architecture that combines windowed self-attention and bidirectional cross-attention with learnable queries to capture multi-scale temporal context. Through the synergy of these data-centric and architectural enhancements, CVA achieves state-of-the-art performance on major VTG benchmarks, including QVHighlights and Charades-STA. Notably, our method achieves a significant improvement of approximately 5 points in Recall@1 (R1) scores over state-of-the-art methods, highlighting its effectiveness in mitigating false negatives.

</details>


### [209] [Amplified Patch-Level Differential Privacy for Free via Random Cropping](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.24695)
*Kaan Durmaz,Jan Schuchardt,Sebastian Schmidt,Stephan Günnemann*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Random cropping adds a third source of stochasticity in DP-SGD by probabilistically excluding localized sensitive content, enabling stronger privacy guarantees; a patch-level model of vision data yields tighter DP bounds and improved privacy-utility empirically across segmentation models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current DP-SGD privacy accounting ignores domain-specific randomness from data augmentation. When sensitive content is localized (e.g., faces, license plates), random cropping can unintentionally hide content from inputs, providing an additional privacy amplification effect that can improve DP guarantees without modifying models or training procedures.

Method: Introduce a patch-level neighboring relation for vision data and derive tight DP-SGD privacy bounds when combined with random cropping. Quantify patch inclusion probability and its interaction with minibatch sampling to obtain a lower effective sampling rate, and validate the theory empirically on multiple segmentation architectures/datasets.

Result: Derived tighter DP bounds for DP-SGD under random cropping via patch-level analysis; empirical experiments show improved privacy-utility trade-offs across several segmentation models and datasets.

Conclusion: Privacy accounting that leverages domain structure and existing randomness (like random cropping) can yield stronger DP guarantees at no extra training cost, suggesting broader applicability in domain-aware DP analyses.

Abstract: Random cropping is one of the most common data augmentation techniques in computer vision, yet the role of its inherent randomness in training differentially private machine learning models has thus far gone unexplored. We observe that when sensitive content in an image is spatially localized, such as a face or license plate, random cropping can probabilistically exclude that content from the model's input. This introduces a third source of stochasticity in differentially private training with stochastic gradient descent, in addition to gradient noise and minibatch sampling. This additional randomness amplifies differential privacy without requiring changes to model architecture or training procedure. We formalize this effect by introducing a patch-level neighboring relation for vision data and deriving tight privacy bounds for differentially private stochastic gradient descent (DP-SGD) when combined with random cropping. Our analysis quantifies the patch inclusion probability and shows how it composes with minibatch sampling to yield a lower effective sampling rate. Empirically, we validate that patch-level amplification improves the privacy-utility trade-off across multiple segmentation architectures and datasets. Our results demonstrate that aligning privacy accounting with domain structure and additional existing sources of randomness can yield stronger guarantees at no additional cost.

</details>


### [210] [Light Cones For Vision: Simple Causal Priors For Visual Hierarchy](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.24753)
*Manglam Kartik,Neel Tushar Shah*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Worldline Slot Attention models objects as persistent spacetime worldlines with multi-level slots, using Lorentzian geometry to encode causal, temporal dependencies. It achieves large accuracy gains over Euclidean baselines (0.078 vs. 0.33 random) and surpasses hyperbolic embeddings across three datasets (0.479–0.661), with strong reproducibility and a small parameter budget (~11K).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard vision representations treat objects as isolated Euclidean points and fail to capture hierarchical structure; there is a need for geometric inductive biases that encode causal, temporal dependencies to support parts-whole hierarchies.

Method: Objects are modeled as trajectories through spacetime with multiple slots per object at different hierarchy levels, sharing spatial position but differing in temporal coordinates. The study compares Euclidean vs Lorentzian worldlines and benchmarks against hyperbolic embeddings, evaluating on three datasets with multiple runs.

Result: Euclidean worldlines underperform (0.078 accuracy, below random 0.33). Lorentzian worldlines achieve 0.479–0.661 accuracy across three datasets, with >6x improvement and consistent replication over 20+ runs. Lorentzian geometry also outperforms hyperbolic embeddings, indicating causal structure is superior for visual hierarchies. The model uses ~11K parameters; code available at the provided URL.

Conclusion: Hierarchical object discovery requires geometric structure that encodes asymmetric causality, which is naturally captured by Lorentzian light-cone geometry. This inductive bias is absent in Euclidean space but aligns with temporal dependencies essential for parts-to-whole hierarchies; the approach achieves strong performance with a small parameter budget.

Abstract: Standard vision models treat objects as independent points in Euclidean space, unable to capture hierarchical structure like parts within wholes. We introduce Worldline Slot Attention, which models objects as persistent trajectories through spacetime worldlines, where each object has multiple slots at different hierarchy levels sharing the same spatial position but differing in temporal coordinates. This architecture consistently fails without geometric structure: Euclidean worldlines achieve 0.078 level accuracy, below random chance (0.33), while Lorentzian worldlines achieve 0.479-0.661 across three datasets: a 6x improvement replicated over 20+ independent runs. Lorentzian geometry also outperforms hyperbolic embeddings showing visual hierarchies require causal structure (temporal dependency) rather than tree structure (radial branching). Our results demonstrate that hierarchical object discovery requires geometric structure encoding asymmetric causality, an inductive bias absent from Euclidean space but natural to Lorentzian light cones, achieved with only 11K parameters. The code is available at: https://github.com/iclrsubmissiongram/loco.

</details>

### [211] [Persistence-based topological optimization: a survey](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.24613)
*Mathieu Carriere,Yuichi Ike,Théo Lacombe,Naoki Nishikawa*

Main category: cs.CG

TL;DR: Survey of gradient-based optimization for persistence-based losses in topological data analysis, covering theory, algorithms, and applications, with an accompanying open-source library.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enable principled optimization of topological descriptors (persistence) within ML and optimization frameworks, addressing differentiability, stability, and scalability concerns.

Method: Review the theoretical foundations of persistent homology, discuss differentiable or differentiable-surrogate approaches to persistence descriptors, outline algorithmic strategies for gradient-based optimization, and illustrate practical applications; provide an open-source library implementing these approaches.

Result: Synthesize the current state of differentiable persistence-based optimization, clarify mathematical and computational properties, and offer a usable software resource and primer for researchers and practitioners.

Conclusion: Differentiable persistence-based losses form a maturing toolkit enabling integration of topological priors into ML, with ongoing developments in theory and computation; the survey provides a comprehensive guide and practical starting point.

Abstract: Computational topology provides a tool, persistent homology, to extract quantitative descriptors from structured objects (images, graphs, point clouds, etc). These descriptors can then be involved in optimization problems, typically as a way to incorporate topological priors or to regularize machine learning models. This is usually achieved by minimizing adequate, topologically-informed losses based on these descriptors, which, in turn, naturally raises theoretical and practical questions about the possibility of optimizing such loss functions using gradient-based algorithms. This has been an active research field in the topological data analysis community over the last decade, and various techniques have been developed to enable optimization of persistence-based loss functions with gradient descent schemes. This survey presents the current state of this field, covering its theoretical foundations, the algorithmic aspects, and showcasing practical uses in several applications. It includes a detailed introduction to persistence theory and, as such, aims at being accessible to mathematicians and data scientists newcomers to the field. It is accompanied by an open-source library which implements the different approaches covered in this survey, providing a convenient playground for researchers to get familiar with the field.

</details>
